<p><b>Sexual orientation</b> is an enduring pattern of <a href="/wiki/Romance_(love)" title="Romance (love)">romantic</a> or <a href="/wiki/Sexual_attraction" title="Sexual attraction">sexual attraction</a> (or a combination of these) to persons of the opposite <a href="/wiki/Sex" title="Sex">sex</a> or <a href="/wiki/Gender" title="Gender">gender</a>, the same sex or gender, or to both sexes or more than one gender. These attractions are generally subsumed under <a href="/wiki/Heterosexuality" title="Heterosexuality">heterosexuality</a>, <a href="/wiki/Homosexuality" title="Homosexuality">homosexuality</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Bisexuality" title="Bisexuality">bisexuality</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AmPsycholAssn-whatis_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-AmPsycholAssn-whatis-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AmPsychiAssn-Sexual_orientation_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-AmPsychiAssn-Sexual_orientation-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AmPsycholAssn-definitions_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-AmPsycholAssn-definitions-3">[3]</a></sup> while <a href="/wiki/Asexuality" title="Asexuality">asexuality</a> (the lack of sexual attraction to others) is sometimes identified as the fourth category.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sex_and_society_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sex_and_society-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bogaert_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bogaert-5">[5]</a></sup>
</p><p>These categories are aspects of the more nuanced nature of <a href="/wiki/Sexual_identity" title="Sexual identity">sexual identity</a> and terminology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AmPsycholAssn-definitions_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-AmPsycholAssn-definitions-3">[3]</a></sup> For example, people may use other <a href="/wiki/Label_(sociology)" title="Label (sociology)">labels</a>, such as <i><a href="/wiki/Pansexuality" title="Pansexuality">pansexual</a></i> or <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polysexuality" title="Polysexuality">polysexual</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AmPsycholAssn-definitions_3-2"><a href="#cite_note-AmPsycholAssn-definitions-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Firestein_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-Firestein-6">[6]</a></sup> or none at all.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AmPsycholAssn-whatis_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-AmPsycholAssn-whatis-1">[1]</a></sup> According to the <a href="/wiki/American_Psychological_Association" title="American Psychological Association">American Psychological Association</a>, sexual orientation "also refers to a person's sense of identity based on those attractions, related behaviors, and membership in a community of others who share those attractions".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AmPsycholAssn-whatis_1-2"><a href="#cite_note-AmPsycholAssn-whatis-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Calif-amici_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-Calif-amici-7">[7]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Androphilia_and_gynephilia" title="Androphilia and gynephilia"><i>Androphilia</i> and <i>gynephilia</i></a> are terms used in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Behavioral_science" title="Behavioral science">behavioral science</a> to describe sexual orientation as an alternative to a <a href="/wiki/Gender_binary" title="Gender binary">gender binary</a> conceptualization. <i>Androphilia</i> describes sexual attraction to <a href="/wiki/Masculinity" title="Masculinity">masculinity</a>; <i>gynephilia</i> describes the sexual attraction to <a href="/wiki/Femininity" title="Femininity">femininity</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-schmidt2010_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-schmidt2010-8">[8]</a></sup> The term <i>sexual preference</i> largely overlaps with sexual orientation, but is generally distinguished in psychological research.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Preference_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Preference-9">[9]</a></sup> A person who identifies as bisexual, for example, may sexually prefer one sex over the other.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rosario_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rosario-10">[10]</a></sup> <i>Sexual preference</i> may also suggest a degree of voluntary choice,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Preference_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-Preference-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> whereas  sexual orientation is not a choice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pediatrics2004_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-pediatrics2004-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kersey-Matusiak_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kersey-Matusiak-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lamanna_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lamanna-15">[15]</a></sup>
</p><p>Scientists do not know the exact cause of sexual orientation, but they theorize that it is caused by a complex interplay of <a href="/wiki/Genetics" title="Genetics">genetic</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hormonal" title="Hormonal">hormonal</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Environment_and_sexual_orientation" title="Environment and sexual orientation">environmental influences</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pediatrics2004_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-pediatrics2004-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lamanna_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-Lamanna-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stuart_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stuart-16">[16]</a></sup> Although no single theory on the cause of sexual orientation has yet gained widespread support, scientists favor <a href="/wiki/Biology_and_sexual_orientation" title="Biology and sexual orientation">biologically based theories</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pediatrics2004_13-2"><a href="#cite_note-pediatrics2004-13">[13]</a></sup> There is considerably more evidence supporting nonsocial, biological causes of sexual orientation than social ones, especially for males.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bailey_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bailey-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LeVay_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-LeVay-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Balthazart_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Balthazart-19">[19]</a></sup> There is no substantive evidence which suggests parenting or early childhood experiences play a role with regard to sexual orientation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rcp2007_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-rcp2007-20">[20]</a></sup> Across cultures, most people are heterosexual, with a minority of people having a homosexual or bisexual orientation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bailey_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bailey-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LeVay_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-LeVay-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 8">: 8 </span></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Balthazart_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-Balthazart-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 9–10">: 9–10 </span></sup> A person's sexual orientation can be anywhere on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heterosexual-homosexual_continuum" title="Heterosexual-homosexual continuum">a continuum</a>, from exclusive attraction to the opposite sex to exclusive attraction to the same sex.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AmPsycholAssn-whatis_1-3"><a href="#cite_note-AmPsycholAssn-whatis-1">[1]</a></sup>
</p><p>Sexual orientation is studied primarily within <a href="/wiki/Biology" title="Biology">biology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Neuroscience" title="Neuroscience">neuroscience</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Psychology" title="Psychology">psychology</a> (including <a href="/wiki/Sexology" title="Sexology">sexology</a>), but it is also a subject area in <a href="/wiki/Sociology" title="Sociology">sociology</a>, <a href="/wiki/History" title="History">history</a> (including <a href="/wiki/Social_constructionism" title="Social constructionism">social constructionist</a> perspectives), and <a href="/wiki/Law" title="Law">law</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cruz_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cruz-21">[21]</a></sup>
</p>
<div aria-labelledby="mw-toc-heading" class="toc" id="toc" role="navigation"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2 id="mw-toc-heading">Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Definitions_and_distinguishing_from_sexual_identity_and_behavior">Definitions and distinguishing from sexual identity and behavior</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sexual_orientation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Definitions and distinguishing from sexual identity and behavior">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="General">General</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sexual_orientation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: General">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Sexual_identity" title="Sexual identity">Sexual identity</a>, <a href="/wiki/Human_sexual_activity" title="Human sexual activity">Human sexual activity</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Situational_sexual_behavior" title="Situational sexual behavior">Situational sexual behavior</a></div>
<p>Sexual orientation is traditionally defined as including <a href="/wiki/Heterosexuality" title="Heterosexuality">heterosexuality</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bisexuality" title="Bisexuality">bisexuality</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Homosexual_orientation" title="Homosexual orientation">homosexuality</a>, while <a href="/wiki/Asexuality" title="Asexuality">asexuality</a> is considered the fourth category of sexual orientation by some researchers and has been defined as the absence of a traditional sexual orientation. An asexual has little to no sexual attraction to people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sex_and_society_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sex_and_society-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bogaert_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bogaert-5">[5]</a></sup> It may be considered a lack of a sexual orientation,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bogaert2004_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bogaert2004-22">[22]</a></sup> and there is significant debate over whether or not it is a sexual orientation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sex_and_society_4-2"><a href="#cite_note-Sex_and_society-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bogaert_5-2"><a href="#cite_note-Bogaert-5">[5]</a></sup>
</p><p>Most definitions of sexual orientation include a psychological component, such as the direction of an individual's erotic desires, or a behavioral component, which focuses on the sex of the individual's sexual partner/s. Some people prefer simply to follow an individual's self-definition or <a href="/wiki/Identity_(social_science)" title="Identity (social science)">identity</a>. Scientific and professional understanding is that "the core attractions that form the basis for adult sexual orientation typically emerge between middle childhood and early adolescence".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AmPsycholAssn-whatis_1-4"><a href="#cite_note-AmPsycholAssn-whatis-1">[1]</a></sup> Sexual orientation differs from sexual identity in that it encompasses relationships with others, while sexual identity is a concept of self.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/American_Psychological_Association" title="American Psychological Association">American Psychological Association</a> states that "[s]exual orientation refers to an enduring pattern of emotional, romantic, and/or sexual attractions to men, women, or both sexes" and that "[t]his range of behaviors and attractions has been described in various cultures and nations throughout the world. Many cultures use identity labels to describe people who express these attractions. In the United States, the most frequent labels are <a href="/wiki/Lesbian" title="Lesbian">lesbians</a> (women attracted to women), <a href="/wiki/Gay_men" title="Gay men">gay men</a> (men attracted to men), and bisexual people (men or women attracted to both sexes). However, some people may use different labels or none at all". They additionally state that sexual orientation "is distinct from other components of sex and gender, including biological sex (the anatomical, physiological, and genetic characteristics associated with being male or female), gender identity (the psychological sense of being male or female), and social gender role (the cultural norms that define feminine and masculine behavior)".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AmPsycholAssn-whatis_1-5"><a href="#cite_note-AmPsycholAssn-whatis-1">[1]</a></sup>
</p><p>Sexual identity and <a href="/wiki/Human_sexual_activity" title="Human sexual activity">sexual behavior</a> are closely related to sexual orientation, but they are distinguished, with sexual identity referring to an individual's conception of themselves, behavior referring to actual sexual acts performed by the individual, and orientation referring to "fantasies, attachments and longings."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> Individuals may or may not express their sexual orientation in their behaviors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AmPsycholAssn-whatis_1-6"><a href="#cite_note-AmPsycholAssn-whatis-1">[1]</a></sup> People who have a <a href="/wiki/Non-heterosexual" title="Non-heterosexual">non-heterosexual</a> sexual orientation that does not align with their sexual identity are sometimes referred to as '<a href="/wiki/Closeted" title="Closeted">closeted</a>'. The term may, however, reflect a certain cultural context and particular stage of transition in societies which are gradually dealing with integrating sexual minorities. In studies related to sexual orientation, when dealing with the degree to which a person's <a href="/wiki/Sexual_attraction" title="Sexual attraction">sexual attractions</a>, behaviors and identity match, scientists usually use the terms <i>concordance</i> or <i>discordance.</i> Thus, a woman who is attracted to other women, but calls herself heterosexual and only has sexual relations with men, can be said to experience discordance between her sexual orientation (homosexual or lesbian) and her sexual identity and behaviors (heterosexual).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Concordance/discordance_in_SO_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-Concordance/discordance_in_SO-24">[24]</a></sup>
</p><p><i>Sexual identity</i> may also be used to describe a person's perception of their own <i>sex</i>, rather than sexual orientation. The term <i>sexual preference</i> has a similar meaning to <i>sexual orientation</i>, and the two terms are often used interchangeably, but the American Psychological Association states <i>sexual preference</i> suggests a degree of voluntary choice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Preference_9-2"><a href="#cite_note-Preference-9">[9]</a></sup> The term has been listed by the American Psychological Association's Committee on Gay and Lesbian Concerns as a wording that advances a "heterosexual bias".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Preference_9-3"><a href="#cite_note-Preference-9">[9]</a></sup> The term <i>sexual orientation</i> was introduced by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sexologist" title="Sexologist">sexologist</a> <a href="/wiki/John_Money" title="John Money">John Money</a> in place of <i>sexual preference</i>, arguing that attraction is not necessarily a matter of free choice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-John_Money,_Ph.D_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-John_Money,_Ph.D-25">[25]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Androphilia.2C_gynephilia.2C_and_other_terms"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Androphilia,_gynephilia,_and_other_terms">Androphilia, gynephilia, and other terms</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sexual_orientation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Androphilia, gynephilia, and other terms">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Androphilia_and_gynephilia" title="Androphilia and gynephilia">Androphilia and gynephilia</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Attraction_to_transgender_people" title="Attraction to transgender people">Attraction to transgender people</a></div>
<p><i>Androphilia</i> and <i>gynephilia</i> (or <i>gynecophilia</i>) are terms used in behavioral science to describe sexual attraction, as an alternative to a homosexual and heterosexual conceptualization. They are used for identifying a subject's object of attraction without attributing a <a href="/wiki/Sex_assignment" title="Sex assignment">sex assignment</a> or <a href="/wiki/Gender_identity" title="Gender identity">gender identity</a> to the subject. Related terms such as <i><a href="/wiki/Pansexuality" title="Pansexuality">pansexual</a></i> and <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polysexuality" title="Polysexuality">polysexual</a></i> do not make any such assignations to the subject.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Firestein_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-Firestein-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sex_and_society2_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sex_and_society2-26">[26]</a></sup> People may also use terms such as <i><a href="/wiki/Queer" title="Queer">queer</a></i>, <i>pansensual,</i> <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polyfidelitous" title="Polyfidelitous">polyfidelitous</a>,</i> <i>ambisexual,</i> or personalized identities such as <i>byke</i> or <i>biphilic</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Firestein_6-2"><a href="#cite_note-Firestein-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p><p>
Using <i>androphilia</i> and <i>gynephilia</i> can avoid confusion and offense when describing people in non-western cultures, as well as when describing intersex and transgender people. Psychiatrist <a href="/wiki/Anil_Aggrawal" title="Anil Aggrawal">Anil Aggrawal</a> explains that androphilia, along with gynephilia,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Aggrawal2008_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-Aggrawal2008-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (August 2021)">page needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p><blockquote><p>is needed to overcome immense difficulties in characterizing the sexual orientation of trans men and trans women. For instance, it is difficult to decide whether a trans man erotically attracted to males is a heterosexual female or a homosexual male; or a trans woman erotically attracted to females is a heterosexual male or a lesbian female. Any attempt to classify them may not only cause confusion but arouse offense among the affected subjects. In such cases, while defining sexual attraction, it is best to focus on the object of their attraction rather than on the sex or gender of the subject.</p></blockquote><p>Sexologist <a href="/wiki/Milton_Diamond" title="Milton Diamond">Milton Diamond</a> writes, "The terms heterosexual, homosexual, and bisexual are better used as adjectives, not nouns, and are better applied to behaviors, not people. This usage is particularly advantageous when discussing the partners of transsexual or intersexed individuals. These newer terms also do not carry the social weight of the former ones."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-diamond2010_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-diamond2010-28">[28]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some researchers advocate use of the terminology to avoid <a href="/wiki/Bias" title="Bias">bias</a> inherent in <a href="/wiki/Western_culture" title="Western culture">Western</a> conceptualizations of human sexuality. Writing about the <a href="/wiki/Samoa" title="Samoa">Samoan</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Fa%27afafine" title="Fa'afafine">fa'afafine</a></i> demographic, sociologist Johanna Schmidt writes that in cultures where a <a href="/wiki/Third_gender" title="Third gender">third gender</a> is recognized, a term like "homosexual transsexual" does not align with cultural categories.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-schmidt2001_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-schmidt2001-29">[29]</a></sup>
</p><p><i><a href="/wiki/Same_gender_loving" title="Same gender loving">Same gender loving</a></i>, or <i>SGL</i>, is a term adopted by some <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/African-American" title="African-American">African-Americans</a>, meant as a culturally affirming homosexual identity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some researchers, such as <a href="/wiki/Bruce_Bagemihl" title="Bruce Bagemihl">Bruce Bagemihl</a>, have criticized certain ways the labels "heterosexual" and "homosexual" have been used for transgender people, writing, "...the point of reference for 'heterosexual' or 'homosexual' orientation in this nomenclature is solely the individual's genetic sex prior to reassignment (see for example, Blanchard et al. 1987, Coleman and Bockting, 1988, Blanchard, 1989).  These labels thereby ignore the individual's personal sense of gender identity taking precedence over biological sex, rather than the other way around." Bagemihl goes on to take issue with the way this terminology makes it easy to claim transsexuals are really homosexual males seeking to escape from stigma.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bagemihl_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-bagemihl-31">[31]</a></sup>
</p><p>Terms have been proposed for sexual attraction to a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Assigned_male_at_birth" title="Assigned male at birth">person born male</a> with a feminine gender expression, including <i>gynandromorphophilia</i> (adjective: <i>gynandromorphophilic</i>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blaney-Krueger2014_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-Blaney-Krueger2014-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Patterson-2020_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-Patterson-2020-33">[33]</a></sup> and <i>gynemimetophilia</i> (adj.: <i>gynemimetophilic</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Money-Lamacz-1984_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-Money-Lamacz-1984-34">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blaney-Krueger2014_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-Blaney-Krueger2014-32">[32]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Gender.2C_transgender.2C_cisgender.2C_and_conformance"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Gender,_transgender,_cisgender,_and_conformance">Gender, transgender, cisgender, and conformance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sexual_orientation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Gender, transgender, cisgender, and conformance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1097763485">.mw-parser-output .ambox{border:1px solid #a2a9b1;border-left:10px solid #36c;background-color:#fbfbfb;box-sizing:border-box}.mw-parser-output .ambox+link+.ambox,.mw-parser-output .ambox+link+style+.ambox,.mw-parser-output .ambox+link+link+.ambox,.mw-parser-output .ambox+.mw-empty-elt+link+.ambox,.mw-parser-output .ambox+.mw-empty-elt+link+style+.ambox,.mw-parser-output .ambox+.mw-empty-elt+link+link+.ambox{margin-top:-1px}html body.mediawiki .mw-parser-output .ambox.mbox-small-left{margin:4px 1em 4px 0;overflow:hidden;width:238px;border-collapse:collapse;font-size:88%;line-height:1.25em}.mw-parser-output .ambox-speedy{border-left:10px solid #b32424;background-color:#fee7e6}.mw-parser-output .ambox-delete{border-left:10px solid #b32424}.mw-parser-output .ambox-content{border-left:10px solid #f28500}.mw-parser-output .ambox-style{border-left:10px solid #fc3}.mw-parser-output .ambox-move{border-left:10px solid #9932cc}.mw-parser-output .ambox-protection{border-left:10px solid #a2a9b1}.mw-parser-output .ambox .mbox-text{border:none;padding:0.25em
