<p>One person may presume knowledge of another person's sexual orientation based upon perceived characteristics, such as appearance, clothing, voice (c.f. <a href="/wiki/Gay_male_speech" title="Gay male speech">Gay male speech</a>), and accompaniment by and behavior with other people. The attempt to detect sexual orientation in social situations is sometimes colloquially known as <a href="/wiki/Gaydar" title="Gaydar">gaydar</a>; some studies have found that guesses based on face photos perform better than chance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup> 2015 research suggests that "gaydar" is an alternate label for using <a href="/wiki/LGBT_stereotypes" title="LGBT stereotypes">LGBT stereotypes</a> to infer orientation, and that face-shape is not an accurate indication of orientation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cox_et_al._(2015)_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cox_et_al._(2015)-124">[124]</a></sup>
</p><p>Perceived sexual orientation may affect how a person is treated. For instance, in the United States, the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Bureau_of_Investigation" title="Federal Bureau of Investigation">FBI</a> reported that 15.6% of <a href="/wiki/Hate_crime" title="Hate crime">hate crimes</a> reported to police in 2004 were "because of a sexual-orientation bias".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FBI_crime_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-FBI_crime-125">[125]</a></sup> Under the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">UK</a> <a href="/wiki/Employment_Equality_(Sexual_Orientation)_Regulations_2003" title="Employment Equality (Sexual Orientation) Regulations 2003">Employment Equality (Sexual Orientation) Regulations 2003</a>, as explained by Advisory, Conciliation and Arbitration Service,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup> "workers or job applicants must not be treated less favourably because of their sexual orientation, their perceived sexual orientation or because they associate with someone of a particular sexual orientation".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup>
</p><p>In Euro-American cultures, norms, values, traditions and laws facilitate heterosexuality,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup> including constructs of marriage and family.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Garnets,_L._2003_114-1"><a href="#cite_note-Garnets,_L._2003-114">[114]</a></sup> Efforts are being made to change prejudiced attitudes, and legislation is being passed to promote equality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Santaemilia,_J._2008_pp._181–98_119-3"><a href="#cite_note-Santaemilia,_J._2008_pp._181–98-119">[119]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some other cultures do not recognize a homosexual/heterosexual/bisexual distinction. It is common to distinguish a person's sexuality according to their sexual role (active/passive; insertive/penetrated). In this distinction, the passive role is typically associated with femininity or inferiority, while the active role is typically associated with masculinity or superiority.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Santaemilia,_J._2008_pp._181–98_119-4"><a href="#cite_note-Santaemilia,_J._2008_pp._181–98-119">[119]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cardoso,_F.L._2005_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cardoso,_F.L._2005-130">[130]</a></sup> For example, an investigation of a small Brazilian fishing village revealed three sexual categories for men: men who have sex only with men (consistently in a passive role), men who have sex only with women, and men who have sex with women and men (consistently in an active role).  While men who consistently occupied the passive role were recognized as a distinct group by locals, men who have sex with only women, and men who have sex with women and men, were not differentiated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cardoso,_F.L._2005_130-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cardoso,_F.L._2005-130">[130]</a></sup> Little is known about same-sex attracted females, or sexual behavior between females in these cultures.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Racism_and_ethnically_relevant_support">Racism and ethnically relevant support</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sexual_orientation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Racism and ethnically relevant support">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/African-American_culture_and_sexual_orientation" title="African-American culture and sexual orientation">African-American culture and sexual orientation</a></div>
<p>In the United States, non-Caucasian LGBT individuals may find themselves in a double minority, where they are neither fully accepted or understood by mainly Caucasian LGBT communities, nor are they accepted by their own ethnic group.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Masequesmay,_G._2003_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-Masequesmay,_G._2003-132">[132]</a></sup> Many people experience racism in the dominant LGBT community where racial stereotypes merge with gender stereotypes, such that Asian-American LGBTs are viewed as more passive and feminine, while African-American LGBTs are viewed as more masculine and aggressive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Garnets,_L._2003_114-2"><a href="#cite_note-Garnets,_L._2003-114">[114]</a></sup> There are a number of culturally specific support networks for LGBT individuals active in the United States. For example, "Ô-Môi" for Vietnamese American queer females.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Masequesmay,_G._2003_132-1"><a href="#cite_note-Masequesmay,_G._2003-132">[132]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Religion">Religion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sexual_orientation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Religion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/LGBT_matters_and_religion" title="LGBT matters and religion">LGBT matters and religion</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Religion_and_homosexuality" title="Religion and homosexuality">Religion and homosexuality</a></div>
<p>Sexuality in the context of religion is often a controversial subject, especially that of sexual orientation. In the past, various sects have viewed homosexuality from a negative point of view and had punishments for same-sex relationships. In modern times, an increasing number of religions and religious denominations accept homosexuality. It is possible to integrate sexual identity and religious identity, depending on the interpretation of religious texts.
</p><p>Some religious organizations object to the concept of sexual orientation entirely.  In the 2014 revision of the code of ethics of the American Association of Christian Counselors, members are forbidden to "describe or reduce human identity and nature to sexual orientation or reference," even while counselors must acknowledge the client's fundamental right to self-determination.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AACC_coe_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-AACC_coe-133">[133]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Internet_and_media">Internet and media</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sexual_orientation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Internet and media">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/LGBT_stereotypes" title="LGBT stereotypes">LGBT stereotypes</a></div>
<p>The Internet has influenced sexual orientation in two ways: it is a common mode of discourse on the subject of sexual orientation and sexual identity, and therefore shapes popular conceptions;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Santaemilia,_J._2008_pp._181–98_119-5"><a href="#cite_note-Santaemilia,_J._2008_pp._181–98-119">[119]</a></sup> and it allows anonymous attainment of sexual partners, as well as facilitates communication and connection between greater numbers of people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Demographics">Demographics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sexual_orientation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Demographics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Demographics_of_sexual_orientation" title="Demographics of sexual orientation">Demographics of sexual orientation</a></div>
<p>Modern scientific surveys find that, across cultures, most people report a heterosexual orientation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bailey_17-5"><a href="#cite_note-Bailey-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LeVay_18-2"><a href="#cite_note-LeVay-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 8">: 8 </span></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Balthazart_19-2"><a href="#cite_note-Balthazart-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 9–10">: 9–10 </span></sup> Bisexuality comes in varying degrees of relative attraction to the same or opposite sex.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bailey_17-6"><a href="#cite_note-Bailey-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LeVay_18-3"><a href="#cite_note-LeVay-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 8–9">: 8–9 </span></sup> Men are more likely to be exclusively homosexual than to be equally attracted to both sexes, while the opposite is true for women.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bailey_17-7"><a href="#cite_note-Bailey-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LeVay_18-4"><a href="#cite_note-LeVay-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 8–9">: 8–9 </span></sup>
</p><p>Surveys in Western cultures find, on average, that about 93% of men and 87% of women identify as completely heterosexual, 4% of men and 10% of women as mostly heterosexual, 0.5% of men and 1% of women as evenly bisexual, 0.5% of men and 0.5% of women as mostly homosexual, and 2% of men and 0.5% of women as completely homosexual.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bailey_17-8"><a href="#cite_note-Bailey-17">[17]</a></sup> An analysis of 67 studies found that the lifetime prevalence of sex between men (regardless of orientation) was 3-5% for East Asia, 6-12% for South and South East Asia, 6-15% for Eastern Europe, and 6-20% for Latin America.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/International_HIV/AIDS_Alliance" title="International HIV/AIDS Alliance">International HIV/AIDS Alliance</a> estimates a worldwide prevalence of <a href="/wiki/Men_who_have_sex_with_men" title="Men who have sex with men">men who have sex with men</a> between 3 and 16%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-International_HIV/AIDS_Alliance_2003_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-International_HIV/AIDS_Alliance_2003-136">[136]</a></sup>
</p><p>The relative percentage of the population that reports a homosexual or bisexual orientation can vary with different methodologies and selection criteria. A 1998 report stated that these statistical findings are in the range of 2.8 to 9% for males, and 1 to 5% for females for the United States<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup> – this figure can be as high as 12% for some large cities and as low as 1% for rural areas.
</p><p>A small percentage of people are not sexually attracted to anyone (<a href="/wiki/Asexuality" title="Asexuality">asexuality</a>). A study in 2004 placed the prevalence of asexuality at 1%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bogaert2006_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bogaert2006-138">[138]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cnn_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-cnn-139">[139]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Kinsey_data">Kinsey data</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sexual_orientation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Kinsey data">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In <i><a href="/wiki/Kinsey_Reports" title="Kinsey Reports">Sexual Behavior in the Human Male</a></i> (1948) and <i>Sexual Behavior in the Human Female</i> (1953), by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alfred_C._Kinsey" title="Alfred C. Kinsey">Alfred C. Kinsey</a> et al., people were asked to rate themselves on a <a href="/wiki/Kinsey_scale" title="Kinsey scale">scale</a> from completely heterosexual to completely homosexual. Kinsey reported that when the individuals' behavior, as well as their identity, are analyzed, a significant number of people appeared to be at least somewhat bisexual – i.e., they have some attraction to either sex, although usually one sex is preferred. Kinsey's methods have been criticized as flawed, particularly with regard to the randomness of his sample population, which included prison inmates, male prostitutes and those who willingly participated in discussion of previously taboo sexual topics. Nevertheless, <a href="/wiki/Paul_Gebhard" title="Paul Gebhard">Paul Gebhard</a>, subsequent director of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kinsey_Institute_for_Sex_Research" title="Kinsey Institute for Sex Research">Kinsey Institute for Sex Research</a>, reexamined the data in the <a href="/wiki/Kinsey_Reports" title="Kinsey Reports">Kinsey Reports</a> and concluded that removing the prison inmates and prostitutes barely affected the results.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MDOG_140-0"><a href="#cite_note-MDOG-140">[140]</a></sup> More recent researchers believe that Kinsey overestimated the rate of same-sex attraction because of flaws in his sampling methods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bailey_17-9"><a href="#cite_note-Bailey-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Balthazart_19-3"><a href="#cite_note-Balthazart-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 9">: 9 </span></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lehmiller_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lehmiller-141">[141]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 147">: 147 </span></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Social_constructionism">Social constructionism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sexual_orientation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Social constructionism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Queer_theory" title="Queer theory">Queer theory</a></div>
<p>Because sexual orientation is complex, some academics and researchers, especially in <a href="/wiki/Queer_studies" title="Queer studies">queer studies</a>, have argued that it is a historical and <a href="/wiki/Social_constructionism" title="Social constructionism">social construction</a>. In 1976, philosopher and historian <a href="/wiki/Michel_Foucault" title="Michel Foucault">Michel Foucault</a> argued in <i><a href="/wiki/The_History_of_Sexuality" title="The History of Sexuality">The History of Sexuality</a></i> that homosexuality as an identity did not exist in the eighteenth century; that people instead spoke of "sodomy," which referred to sexual acts. Sodomy was a crime that was often ignored, but sometimes punished severely under <a href="/wiki/Sodomy_law" title="Sodomy law">sodomy laws</a>. He wrote, "'Sexuality' is an invention of the modern state, the industrial revolution, and capitalism."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup> Other scholars argue that there are significant continuities between ancient and modern homosexuality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Norton_2016_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-Norton_2016-143">[143]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Boswell_1989_17–36_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-Boswell_1989_17–36-144">[144]</a></sup> The philosopher of science <a href="/wiki/Michael_Ruse" title="Michael Ruse">Michael Ruse</a> has stated that the social constructionist approach, which is influenced by Foucault, is based on a selective reading of the historical record that confuses the existence of homosexual people with the way in which they are labelled or treated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup>
</p><p>In much of the modern world, <a href="/wiki/Sexual_identity" title="Sexual identity">sexual identity</a> is defined based on the sex of one's partner. In some parts of the world, however, sexuality is often socially defined based on sexual roles, whether one is a penetrator or is penetrated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHOMSM_146-0"><a href="#cite_note-WHOMSM-146">[146]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup> In Western cultures, people speak meaningfully of gay, lesbian, and bisexual identities and communities. In some other cultures, homosexuality and heterosexual labels do not emphasize an entire social identity or indicate community affiliation based on sexual orientation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some historians and researchers<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too-vague attribution or weasel words. (May 2019)">who?</span></a></i>]</sup> argue that the emotional and affectionate activities associated with sexual-orientation terms such as "gay" and "heterosexual" change significantly over time and across cultural boundaries. For example, in many English-speaking nations, it is assumed that same-sex kissing, particularly between men, is a sign of homosexuality, whereas various types of same-sex kissing are common expressions of friendship in other nations. Also, many modern and historic cultures have formal ceremonies expressing long-term commitment between same-sex friends, even though homosexuality itself is taboo within the cultures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span id="Law.2C_politics_and_theology"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Law,_politics_and_theology">Law, politics and theology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sexual_orientation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Law, politics and theology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Professor Michael King stated, "The conclusion reached by scientists who have investigated the origins and stability of sexual orientation is that it is a human characteristic that is formed early in life, and is resistant to change. Scientific evidence on the origins of homosexuality is considered relevant to theological and social debate because it undermines suggestions that sexual orientation is a choice."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1999, law professor David Cruz wrote that "sexual orientation (and the related concept homosexuality) might plausibly refer to a variety of different attributes, singly or in combination. What is not immediately clear is whether one conception is most suited to all social, legal, and constitutional purposes."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cruz_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cruz-21">[21]</a></sup>
</p>

<li id="cite_note-AmPsycholAssn-definitions-3"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-AmPsycholAssn-definitions_3-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-AmPsycholAssn-definitions_3-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-AmPsycholAssn-definitions_3-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation web cs1"><a class="external text" href="https://www.apa.org/pi/lgbt/resources/sexuality-definitions.pdf" rel="nofollow">"Definitions Related to Sexual Orientation and Gender Diversity in APA Documents"</a> <span class="cs1-format">(PDF)</span>. <a href="/wiki/American_Psychological_Association" title="American Psychological Association">American Psychological Association</a>. 2015. p. 6. <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20210122054116/https://www.apa.org/pi/lgbt/resources/sexuality-definitions.pdf" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> <span class="cs1-format">(PDF)</span> from the original on January 22, 2021<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">February 6,</span> 2020</span>. <q>Sexual orientation refers to the sex of those to whom one is sexually and romantically attracted. [...] [It is] one's enduring sexual attraction to male partners, female partners, or both. Sexual orientation may be heterosexual, same-sex (gay or lesbian), or bisexual. [...] A person may be attracted to men, women, both, neither, or to people who are genderqueer, androgynous, or have other gender identities. Individuals may identify as lesbian, gay, heterosexual, bisexual, queer, pansexual, or asexual, among others. [...] Categories of sexual orientation typically have included attraction to members of one's own sex (gay men or lesbians), attraction to members of the other sex (heterosexuals), and attraction to members of both sexes (bisexuals). While these categories continue to be widely used, research has suggested that sexual orientation does not always appear in such definable categories and instead occurs on a continuum [...]. Some people identify as pansexual or queer in terms of their sexual orientation, which means they define their sexual orientation outside of the gender binary of 'male' and 'female' only.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.btitle=Definitions+Related+to+Sexual+Orientation+and+Gender+Diversity+in+APA+Documents&amp;rft.pages=6&amp;rft.pub=American+Psychological+Association&amp;rft.date=2015&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.apa.org%2Fpi%2Flgbt%2Fresources%2Fsexuality-definitions.pdf&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ASexual+orientation"></span></span>

<li id="cite_note-Lamanna-15"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Lamanna_15-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Lamanna_15-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Lamanna_15-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Lamanna_15-3"><sup><i><b>d</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Lamanna_15-4"><sup><i><b>e</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Lamanna_15-5"><sup><i><b>f</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFLamannaRiedmannStewart2014">Lamanna, Mary Ann; Riedmann, Agnes; Stewart, Susan D (2014). <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=fofaAgAAQBAJ&amp;pg=PA82" rel="nofollow"><i>Marriages, Families, and Relationships: Making Choices in a Diverse Society</i></a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cengage_Learning" title="Cengage Learning">Cengage Learning</a>. p. 82. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-1-305-17689-8" title="Special:BookSources/978-1-305-17689-8"><bdi>978-1-305-17689-8</bdi></a>. <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20161130141623/https://books.google.com/books?id=fofaAgAAQBAJ&amp;pg=PA82" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> from the original on November 30, 2016<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">February 11,</span> 2016</span>. <q>The reason some individuals develop a gay sexual identity has not been definitively established  – nor do we yet understand the development of heterosexuality. The American Psychological Association (APA) takes the position that a variety of factors impact a person's sexuality. The most recent literature from the APA says that sexual orientation is not a choice that can be changed at will, and that sexual orientation is most likely the result of a complex interaction of environmental, cognitive and biological factors...is shaped at an early age...[and evidence suggests] biological, including genetic or inborn hormonal factors, play a significant role in a person's sexuality (American Psychological Association 2010).</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Marriages%2C+Families%2C+and+Relationships%3A+Making+Choices+in+a+Diverse+Society&amp;rft.pages=82&amp;rft.pub=Cengage+Learning&amp;rft.date=2014&amp;rft.isbn=978-1-305-17689-8&amp;rft.aulast=Lamanna&amp;rft.aufirst=Mary+Ann&amp;rft.au=Riedmann%2C+Agnes&amp;rft.au=Stewart%2C+Susan+D&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3DfofaAgAAQBAJ%26pg%3DPA82&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ASexual+orientation"></span></span>
</li>

<li id="cite_note-fluidity-49"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-fluidity_49-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">*<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation journal cs1" id="CITEREFBaileyVaseyDiamondBreedlove2016">Bailey, J. Michael; Vasey, Paul; Diamond, Lisa; <a href="/wiki/Marc_Breedlove" title="Marc Breedlove">Breedlove, S. Marc</a>; Vilain, Eric; Epprecht, Marc (2016). <a class="external text" href="https://www.researchgate.net/publication/301639075" rel="nofollow">"Sexual Orientation, Controversy, and Science"</a>. <i>Psychological Science in the Public Interest</i>. <b>17</b> (2): 45–101. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doi_(identifier)" title="Doi (identifier)">doi</a>:<span class="cs1-lock-free" title="Freely accessible"><a class="external text" href="https://doi.org/10.1177%2F1529100616637616" rel="nofollow">10.1177/1529100616637616</a></span>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PMID_(identifier)" title="PMID (identifier)">PMID</a> <a class="external text" href="//pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/27113562" rel="nofollow">27113562</a>. <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20200611031054/https://www.researchgate.net/publication/301639075" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> from the original on 2020-06-11<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">2019-09-29</span></span>. <q>Sexual fluidity is situation-dependent flexibility in a person’s sexual responsiveness, which makes it possible for some individuals to experience desires for either men or women under certain circumstances regardless of their overall sexual orientation....We expect that in all cultures the vast majority of individuals are sexually predisposed exclusively to the other sex (i.e., heterosexual) and that only a minority of individuals are sexually predisposed (whether exclusively or non-exclusively) to the same sex.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Psychological+Science+in+the+Public+Interest&amp;rft.atitle=Sexual+Orientation%2C+Controversy%2C+and+Science&amp;rft.volume=17&amp;rft.issue=2&amp;rft.pages=45-101&amp;rft.date=2016&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1177%2F1529100616637616&amp;rft_id=info%3Apmid%2F27113562&amp;rft.aulast=Bailey&amp;rft.aufirst=J.+Michael&amp;rft.au=Vasey%2C+Paul&amp;rft.au=Diamond%2C+Lisa&amp;rft.au=Breedlove%2C+S.+Marc&amp;rft.au=Vilain%2C+Eric&amp;rft.au=Epprecht%2C+Marc&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.researchgate.net%2Fpublication%2F301639075&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ASexual+orientation"></span>
<ul><li><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFDennis_CoonJohn_O._Mitterer2012">Dennis Coon; John O. Mitterer (2012). <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=EYwjCQAAQBAJ&amp;pg=PA372" rel="nofollow"><i>Introduction to Psychology: Gateways to Mind and Behavior with Concept Maps and Reviews</i></a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cengage_Learning" title="Cengage Learning">Cengage Learning</a>. p. 372. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-1111833633" title="Special:BookSources/978-1111833633"><bdi>978-1111833633</bdi></a><span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">February 18,</span> 2016</span>. <q>Sexual orientation is a deep part of personal identity and is usually quite stable. Starting with their earliest erotic feelings, most people remember being attracted to either the opposite sex or the same sex. [...] The fact that sexual orientation is usually quite stable doesn't rule out the possibility that for some people sexual behavior may change during the course of a lifetime.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Introduction+to+Psychology%3A+Gateways+to+Mind+and+Behavior+with+Concept+Maps+and+Reviews&amp;rft.pages=372&amp;rft.pub=Cengage+Learning&amp;rft.date=2012&amp;rft.isbn=978-1111833633&amp;rft.au=Dennis+Coon&amp;rft.au=John+O.+Mitterer&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3DEYwjCQAAQBAJ%26pg%3DPA372&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ASexual+orientation"></span></li>
<li><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFEric_AndersonMark_McCormack2016">Eric Anderson; Mark McCormack (2016). <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=7_AgDAAAQBAJ&amp;pg=PA47" rel="nofollow">"Measuring and Surveying Bisexuality"</a>. <i>The Changing Dynamics of Bisexual Men's Lives</i>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Springer_Science_%26_Business_Media" title="Springer Science &amp; Business Media">Springer Science &amp; Business Media</a>. p. 47. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-3-319-29412-4" title="Special:BookSources/978-3-319-29412-4"><bdi>978-3-319-29412-4</bdi></a>. <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20210824071645/https://books.google.com/books?id=7_AgDAAAQBAJ&amp;pg=PA47" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> from the original on August 24, 2021<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">June 22,</span> 2019</span>. <q>[R]esearch suggests that women's sexual orientation is slightly more likely to change than men's (Baumeister 2000; Kinnish et al. 2005). The notion that sexual orientation can change over time is known as <i>sexual fluidity</i>. Even if sexual fluidity exists for some women, it does not mean that the majority of women will change sexual orientations as they age – rather, sexuality is stable over time for the majority of people.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=bookitem&amp;rft.atitle=Measuring+and+Surveying+Bisexuality&amp;rft.btitle=The+Changing+Dynamics+of+Bisexual+Men%27s+Lives&amp;rft.pages=47&amp;rft.pub=Springer+Science+%26+Business+Media&amp;rft.date=2016&amp;rft.isbn=978-3-319-29412-4&amp;rft.au=Eric+Anderson&amp;rft.au=Mark+McCormack&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3D7_AgDAAAQBAJ%26pg%3DPA47&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ASexual+orientation"></span></li></ul>

<li id="cite_note-142"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-142">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=dMAtGvry_IMC&amp;pg=PA32&amp;lpg=PA32&amp;dq=china+heterosexuality+love+western&amp;source=web&amp;ots=PKv2BfyPpd&amp;sig=s43w6PzlNOgefEsjpHViwrxkM6A&amp;hl=en&amp;sa=X&amp;oi=book_result&amp;resnum=1&amp;ct=result" rel="nofollow"><i>Chinese Femininities, Chinese Masculinities: A Reader</i></a> <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20170312212325/https://books.google.com/books?id=dMAtGvry_IMC&amp;pg=PA32&amp;lpg=PA32&amp;dq=china+heterosexuality+love+western&amp;source=web&amp;ots=PKv2BfyPpd&amp;sig=s43w6PzlNOgefEsjpHViwrxkM6A&amp;hl=en&amp;sa=X&amp;oi=book_result&amp;resnum=1&amp;ct=result" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> 2017-03-12 at the <a href="/wiki/Wayback_Machine" title="Wayback Machine">Wayback Machine</a>, by Susan Brownell &amp; Jeffrey N. Wasserstrom (Univ. of Calif. Press, 2002 (<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0-520-22116-8" title="Special:BookSources/0-520-22116-8">0-520-22116-8</a>, <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-520-22116-1" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-520-22116-1">978-0-520-22116-1</a>)). Quote: "The problem with sexuality: Some scholars have argued that maleness and femaleness were not closely linked to sexuality in China. Michel Foucault's <i><a href="/wiki/The_History_of_Sexuality" title="The History of Sexuality">The History of Sexuality</a></i> (which deals primarily with Western civilization and western Europe) began to influence some China scholars in the 1980s. Foucault's insight was to demonstrate that sexuality has a history; it is not fixed psycho-biological drive that is the same for all humans according to their sex, but rather it is a cultural construct inseparable from gender constructs. After unmooring sexuality from biology, he anchored it in history, arguing that this thing we now call sexuality came into existence in the eighteenth-century West and did not exist previously in this form. "Sexuality" is an invention of the modern state, the industrial revolution, and capitalism. Taking this insight as a starting point, scholars have slowly been compiling the history of sexuality in China. The works by Tani Barlow, discussed above, were also foundational in this trend. Barlow observes that, in the West, heterosexuality is the primary site for the production of gender: a woman truly becomes a woman only in relation to a man's heterosexual desire. By contrast, in China before the 1920s the "jia" (linage unit, family) was the primary site for the production of gender: marriage and sexuality were to serve the lineage by producing the next generation of lineage members; personal love and pleasure were secondary to this goal. Barlow argues that this has two theoretical implications: (1) it is not possible to write a Chinese history of heterosexuality, sexuality as an institution, and sexual identities in the European metaphysical sense, and (2) it is not appropriate to ground discussions of Chinese gender processes in the sexed body so central in "Western" gender processes. Here she echoes Furth's argument that, before the early twentieth century, sex-identity grounded on anatomical difference did not hold a central place in Chinese constructions of gender. And she echoes the point illustrated in detail in Sommer's chapter on male homosexuality in the Qing legal code: a man could engage in homosexual behavior without calling into question his manhood so long as his behavior did not threaten the patriarchal Confucian family structure."</span>
</li>

Bloomsbury Academic. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/9781474286923" title="Special:BookSources/9781474286923"><bdi>9781474286923</bdi></a>. <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20210511080439/https://books.google.com/books?id=DkTqDAAAQBAJ" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> from the original on 2021-05-11<span class="reference-accessdate">.
